I've noticed some rare instances of another type of RHM that I'd call "the manager."  Essentially the opposite of the bodyguard, these guys have practically no combat ability and instead provide services like general book-keeping, PR management, etc. to a boss who is typically hyper- competent except for one particular area, or just lazy.  This can often be relegated to generic minion work, but becomes a true RHM when either their boss or the writer values them enough to give them character treatment.  Kyoraku's lieutenant Nanao, Sabertooth's assistant Birdy, and Black Mask's unnamed secretary from "Under the Red Hood" all come to mind as examples.

*On-Screen:* Once the Dragon and the Villain split:  1. Villain backstabs Dragon (Example: "Dragon Ball Z (Saiyan Saga)" - Vegeta [Villain] betrays Nappa [Dragon] by killing him once he becomes paralyzed, therefore making him "useless" (I believe that, in the Japanese version, Vegeta says, "I have no use for a paralyzed Saiyan!" and then proceeds to kill him.))  2. Dragon backstabs Villain and becomes the NEW Main Villain/Big Bad (Example: "Final Fantasy VI" - Kefka Palazzo [Dragon] betrays Emperor Gestahl [Villain] by killing him, becoming the Main Villain for the rest of the game's story.)  3. Dragon backstabs Villain for Heroics (Example: Disney's "The Emperor's New Groove" - Kronk [Dragon] betrays Yzma [Villain] when she reveals she never liked his Spinach Puffs. {*Note: This is a pretty ridiculous reason for the betrayal; but, given that the film is a comedy, it kinda makes sense that Kronk's reason for betraying Yzma are kind of nonsensical.})

I love the moment in The Incredibles when Mirage turns on Syndrome after he's willing to throw her life away to make a point to Mr Incredible, and he spares her. I like when we find out that the right-hand man was secretly the bigger threat than the boss, and they were manipulating the boss into thinking they were in charge, like a devious puppetmaster.  Sometimes this happens when the main villain is having second thoughts and might be redeemable, while the minion is completely dedicated to the cause. Minion from Spy Kids is a good example of this. The boss is all about the big picture and grand ideals, and those can be shaken up, but the toiling, thankless underling keeps reassuring them that they're on the right track. In actuality, the right-hand man has amassed power through their organization and hard work and they're not willing to let go. At some point, the boss has been turned into a figurehead, a puppet of their former subordinate, who used to take orders from them. It's only when the boss's conscience finally get to them and they try to put a stop to their evil plan that they find out that it's too late. They're not calling the shots anymore, and they've been usurped by someone they took for granted. In these cases, the former villain often tries to help the heroes as part of a redemption arc.  The puppetmaster is different from Starscream, because Starscream is openly insubordinate and tries to undermine the leader, but the puppeteer is happy to remain in the shadows as the power behind the throne and let the boss take the fall, while they can play victim of circustance. Is cases when the villain doesn't have a change of heart but is defeated, the good guys think they've won by taking out the boss, but by letting the minion go, they allow the evil to continue. The minion may keep running the organization under the heroes' noses, or they may find a new boss and new organization to manipulate into accomplishing their goals. Think Arnim Zola keeping Hydra running after Red Skull's demise.
on the note of the Dragon part of this talk, I gotta shout out 2 different games for how they both handle the dynamic but in very different ways. Metaphor: Refantazio & Super Paper Mario (for those who don't know where I'm going with the latter hang in there). Obviously massive spoilers for both games under the Read More.    First I'll talk about Super Paper Mario. The game's whole conflict centers around Count Bleck trying to cause multiversal Armageddon using a dark prophecy because he had his own personal Romeo & Juliet plot end with his own father killing his loved one & given how he didn't already have the best life, he just SNAPPED resulting in a "any world where she doesn't exist doesn't deserve to exist itself". To help with this, he ends up helping & recruiting some like-minded people who follow him out of actual loyalty due to how he helped each of them... & Dimentio who he just kinda hires at some point before the start of the game. Now the other 3 as I said are undyingly loyal. He makes no secret of his plan & they are all 100% on board in seeing his vision realized since it means helping HIM. This works out great since as his motives start to wane due to it becoming more & more obvious that his love is actually alive in the form of the companion who's been traveling with the heroes, you can see the path of them all following him after a potential heel-face turn. And then there's Dimentio AKA one of my favorite villains in gaming. This sly fuck was following Bleck for the sole purpose of HIJACKING THE PROPHECY FOR HIS OWN MEANS. You get hints of this as you go through the game but he only reveals his hand RIGHT AS MARIO & CREW FINISH THE BIG FIGHT WITH BLECK! Hell he would have fully succeeded if Dimentio didn't just happen to put Bleck & his lost love IN THE SAME FUCKING ROOM (the MacGuffins you just used on Bleck are explicitly stated to be powered by love)! So this game gets both super loyal Dragons & also a Dragon who betrays the big bad out of pure ambition.  And then there's Metaphor. Holy fuck Metaphor. This game has 3 instances of a Dragon & Big Bad dynamic. You've got Sanctifex Forden & his loyal warrior monk Gideux, actual big bad Louis & the Magnus Brothers, & then later on Louis & Zorba. Forden & Gideux are simple enough. You've got the leader of a corrupted church & his right hand who actually believes in using the teachings to help people so once Forden's plan is fully revealed post-mortem, Gideux has a crisis of faith before being found now running the church you go to to upgrade some of your gear & buy status cures just doing what he can to atone for his mistakes. THE LOUIS DYNAMICS ON THE OTHER HAND. First lets talk Zorba. Dude is a necromancer who is INSANELY loyal to Louis to the point that how you get past his magic barrage in the first dungeon is by using a portrait of his charge as a shield. This man knows fully what Louis' plan is but is also crazy enough to be fully on board with it after dealing with enough discrimination that he just wants to watch the world burn & knows Louis is capable of doing just that. And then there's the Magnus Brothers. Introduced in between the first 2 dungeons, they basically act as Louis' bodyguards in the "we lighten the load" fashion (Louis could solo damn near anyone but also understands how an army can help his goals). The brothers follow him because they want to see the "lesser" tribes get equal rights & the way Louis worded his goals makes them believe that's what he's doing. Jump forward a few story arcs where the brothers had spent at least one dungeon's worth of plot being near our party & they start to question Louis' motives. Then Louis moves to attack an innocent family to make an example while the party confronts him, the brothers go to shield them from the attack because IT'S AN INNOCENT FAMILY, their barrier starts to break, & the older brother pushes the younger out of the way in a sacrifice play with the aftermath of it all resulting in the party gaining their final member in a tragic fashion (with what is hands down my favorite awakening of the protag party power in any ATLUS game)

It takes Amos a long time to settle into being Holden's right-hand man in The Expanse.  On one hand, Holden's a pretty tough guy and Amos is an absolute monster that could go through Holden like a bandsaw through a tomato, but on the other hand, Amos isn't dumb.  He's a competent engineer and almost pathologically analytical in how he approaches most situations. My personal favorite right-hand man (besides Samwise Gamgee of course) has to be the aptly-named Right Hand Man from the Henry Stickmin games. From the very start, it's clear that his only loyalty is to the Toppat Clan and Reginald Copperbottom specifically as the leader. In ItA, there are three real ways it can end for RHM. First, you get the Government Supported Private Investigator ending and he gets arrested, and doesn't show up in CtM. Second, you get Relentless Bounty Hunter or Rapidly Promoted Executive, in which you brutalize him and he comes back with cybernetic upgrades in CtM. Or third, you get Pure Blooded Thief, where nothing really changes for him except the clan not having the Romanian Ruby anymore.  If you pair RPE with Convict Allies from FtC, then you get the Toppat King ending of CtM. If you pair RPE with the Presumed Dead ending of FtC, you get the Toppat 4 Lyfe ending of CtM. In these endings, Reginald recognizes Henry as the rightful leader of the Toppat Clan, and so RHM helps Henry (and Ellie if you went TK instead of T4L) get onto the rocket and into space.  However, if you pair RPE with Ghost Inmate or The Betrayed, RHM *jumps* on the chance to get rid of Henry so that Reginald can stay leader of the Toppat Clan, causing either the Toppat Civil War ending (RPE+GI) or the Revenged ending (RPE+TB).  As for the MBH routes, it's pretty simple. RHM got cybernetic upgrades and is gonna use them to stomp the crap outta Henry for arresting Reginald.  Lastly, we've got the PBT endings.  There are actually four PBT endings even though PBT can only match with three of the FtC endings: PBT+GI, which gives the Jewel Baron ending, PBT+PD for Stickmin Space Resort, and of course PBT+CI, where the ending branches into Toppat Recruits and  Pardoned Pals. In JB, SSR, and PP, RHM spends the entire path trying his damndest to kill Henry for stealing the Ruby. But, in TR, at the very end of the path, he tosses AKs to Henry and Ellie, who take out the guards from the Wall while he takes out Charles' helicopter. He lets the two of them join under the condition that Henry returns the Ruby, demonstrating that his loyalty is, above all else except Reginald, to the Toppat Clan.

Depending the on the genre, a right hand man can also be something other than badass, like just competent. You could have a leader who has ideas or ambitions, while the right-hand man's job is simply to make those ideas and ambitions happen. One person has the charisma and visions to move things forward, while the other has the competency to ensure that the leader's plans actually happen without a hitch. It's often a fun dynamic when the leader is insightful, but scatterbrained and needs their right hand just to remind them what day it is or when their next meeting is scheduled.

One of my favorite "right hand man" pairings was Black and Sabah from _A Practical Guide To Evil._ They have it all: loyalty, synergy between their powers (he gets stronger when he's leading, she gets stronger when she's following orders, and this means she can go all out without worrying about collateral damage). Sabah even has a husband and kids waiting for her at home, and Black mentions that she has a very happy home life. Especially since he blames himself for her death, believing that it was his own failure as a leader that got her killed.

This video just gave me a weird thought. Is a badass still badass when their badassery isn't action-y? I'm writing a fanfic right now where the main character goes through a bunch of trials and such to learn that fighting and other action-hero-y stuff aren't necessary to be a hero. So now I'm wondering if the term badass can follow similar rules. There are certainly badasses discussed here that aren't especially great fighters. Commander Riker isn't a poor fighter by any means, but his badassery has more to do with effective command than fighting. But being a good commander is still action-y. Is an especially competent secretary a badass right-hand man? What about a really competent butler or maid who isn't a battle-butler? What about a really skilled and wise janitor who works with a teacher to help a troubled kid? Or hell, what about a batman/orderly who can't even hold their weapon without trembling, but who serves an officer who is an action-hero badass, and serves them so effectively that the officer keeps putting them up for medals?

The Right Hand Man trope is one I vibe with very intensely. I live with chronic conditions that make life on my own outright impossible, which means participation in my local community is Not Optional. As such, whenever someone shows me care by doing something I can't, I go out of my way to do something I *can* do for them-- namely, cook. I feed people, and listen, and offer little nudges of advice for conflict resolution when appropriate.   When I was still in school, my friends were everyone who didn't already have a clique for whatever reason. If you're someone who missed a class to cry in a bathroom because a mob of kids decided being cruel would entertain them for a few minutes, you found your way to my lunch table sooner or later... because I had a 5 acer bellow and at first sight of someone 'joking around' with a person who isn't laughing and, in fact, is on the brink of tears I would not hesitate to use it. Dropping and octaive and yelling BACK OFF when I saw that crap brought a lot of people into my life who just wanted an ounce of peace.   We all had our difficulties. Nurodivergant, queer, or just Disinterested In The Social Polotics Of The Lunchroom. None of us had the language for it at the time, but we were all treated As If Our Existance Was Wrong in some way, and we all felt that and found our way to a space where That Would Not Be Allowed. We all are people, maybe odd people, who don't need to earn our right to exist unharassed.   But demanding to not be harassed is scary, and not everyone has it in them to just SNAP at a person as a preformance of force. Most of us can't get that loud unless we're actively in pain, and even then... these were all the kids who hid injury and sickness because their parents Would Interrogate Them before granting support or relief, and That Was Terrifying. Better to just go to school, not kick up a fuss, it's probably just a cold.   We'd all gotten used to suffering, because asking for help had burnt each and every one of us. Seeking support ended up with an authority figure investigating for Other Reasons why the child might be seeking help, finding out something innocuous, and Blaming That and Forcing The Kid To Give Up A Coping Mechanism over Actually Helping.   We'd all suffered victim blaming. We all knew our harrassers would face no consequences. We all knew that We were the only ones who would Actually Hear It when someone else needed help.   We were each others right hand men, each for different things. A tiny village, desperately trying to Survive Long Enough to escape our conditions.   Mutual support man, its a good thing.
Best Right Hand man: Ferik Jurgen.
My personal favorite kind of right-hand man is the one who acts more like a partner to the main guy (good or bad). Where on paper yeah the right-hand man is technically subservient to the main guy but if he really wanted to he could go at it alone and hold his own just as well as the main guy. But he chooses to remain at the side of the main guy for any number of reasons from just liking having someone on the same wavelength to talk to or because he understands the concept of strength in numbers. The important part of this kind of right-hand man is that he is basically as equal as he can get compared to the main guy, he isnt a schemer planning on backstabbing nor is he a loyal brute (physical or magical). Hes literally someone who on every level can understand and converse with the main guy. Im not good at examples (i am a filthy ignoramus) but the one i think of the most is the trio of Big Boss/Venom Snake, Ocelot, and Miller. These three guys could all run their own game easily (and they technically do for 9 years).

Warning, spoilers below: Rewatching this has reminded me of Critical Role and in particular The Legend of Vox Machina, since that's how I'm choosing to first experience campaign 1.        I find the Briarwoods a fascinating villain-dragon duo because they're full-on irredeemable pure evil villains *in a genuine, loving relationship.* No manipulation of each other, no backstabs, they care about each other equally and their pure evil villainy comes in part from that love: they care more about each other than they do about the entire world and everyone in it, and actively try to bring apocalyptic-sounding events on the world just to be together forever.  It's weird and slightly unsettling to see a motivation that's usually framed as healthy and/or heroic make *so much sense* as a motivation for pure evil villains.

I just want to say, that this video inspired me to make one of the dnd characters I play. They had a simple design but the underlying question was ‚Äúwhat if the story I told was how the hero became the villain‚Äôs dragon?‚Äù and that whole journey. So, I built up a typical seeming hero.   She came from a commoner background, was raised by her grandparents after her parents passed away (it‚Äôs just dangerous to travel in general for that world, no big revenge), she saved her childhood friend, and a local noble saw her potential and sponsored her for an apprenticeship that allowed her to gain the combat power and skills she starts with. After hearing her childhood might be in danger, she leaps into the adventure to try and save her. Typical hero right?  Well, that noble? Is a terrible person and pressured my character as a teen to accept a contract for the apprenticeship so he could save face for my character saving his daughter (the childhood friend). He was trying to break up that friendship but also did it to manipulate and isolate his daughter more so he could toss her into a cult later on for his own machinations (the daughter had powerful magic potential). My character, knowing this, did negotiate some of the dear but it was mostly to stay in contact with her friend. The noble agreed on the condition that my hero never speak of the deal, especially to his daughter. And so my character left, which had the childhood friend feeling alone and betrayed even with the letters. Then when my character many years later heard her friend was kidnapped by some cult, she rushed to the last known location and this is when she discovers that her childhood friend is part of said cult‚Ä¶.and this is how her journey begins to her eventual possible descent into becoming her childhood friend‚Äôs dragon.   Last I played the pair had their first in person conversation in years. It was awkward but definitely seemed like the childhood friend still cared and were livid my hero told her that the father was to blame for her leaving at all. But my character also made it clear that her loyalty isn‚Äôt to being a hero. She is kind and generous, sure, but her loyalty always has been and always will be to the childhood friend (yes, my hero is in love with the villain). So my character has agreed to start tearing down various leaders around the cult to help the villainous childhood friend rise in power. There is just one snag. By accident, my character has killed the beloved of her childhood friend. Her friend does not know, and has a low chance of discovering it was my hero. But my character cannot possibly let their friend know this, even as the guilt eats them (in all fairness, she wasn‚Äôt aware of the relationship until later). Now my character is working to see if they can gather all the expensive materials to try to resurrect this individual, even knowing that this means she will be revealed as the killer. She‚Äôs made her peace with that but is determined to do right by her childhood friend.   So yeah, it‚Äôs complicated but simple and is my way of telling the story of how the hero became the dragon and then how the villain killed that dragon.  Their relationship was always intended to end in tragedy (which is why my character being in love with their childhood friend is just another way I up the knife twisting, all to make the tragedy more heartbreaking when it happens).

For what it's worth, I'm pretty sure the "Dragon" is a Biblical reference: In the Book of Revelations, Satan is the final boss of evil and "the Dragon" (colloquially called the Antichrist even though the Bible never uses that term) is Satan's avatar to rule the world and tempt humanity away from God.

I have an idea for a game that has a main villain and a right-hand-man.  Their relationship is actually nontraditional.  The villain is the more powerful of the two, and the RHM is the smart one.  The villain sees the RHM as a deep friend or brother, due to how long they've known each other, but the RHM has a more codependency/love for the villain, unable to live without him.  The villain actually is a good person, but the RHM is more psychopathic.  This leads to him using more dark methods to preserve the villain, and eventually partially corrupting him.  It feels like a reverse dragon and villain trope, and a corruption story as well.  The relationship is kept in the dark quite a bit throughout the game, and is seen as loyalty rather than love.

I'd argue that into the spiderverse isn't the best choice to show over the explanation of loner type characters set apart from the supporting cast, and "having to go it alone." The whole point is Miles realizing that he is *not* alone as spiderman and there are other people like him. Its a story of how both a good support system and personal drive and determination can contribute to greatness. I mean, one of the last lines in the movie is "and I'm not the only one. Not by a long shot." Additionally, characters like gwen and peter b feel less transient in Miles' life is because they both grow as characters after meeting him, and influence his growth in return. Plus there's the promise (and later payoff) of them meeting again. If you want an example from this movie of Miles realizing he has to go it alone is the arc with uncle Aaron. It's a realization that he can't trust the one of the most important and influential people in his life - someone he's gone to for advice and comfort. In a different kind of story that betrayal could turn him against the other spideys. But he doesn't. Because the one of the points of that movie (and I'd argue an overarching theme if the series so far) is about community. Why the trope is called "The Dragon": my theory: this is based on fantasy and especially fantasy gaming. The Big Boss is the last battle in the entire story, the End end. Once you kill him, the story is over. Now you could make this the hardest fight in the game, but oddly what you find a lot of the time in games is that it is the second-to-last fight in the game, or the mini boss, which is the hardest, while the actual final boss is comparatively easier. This metaphorically is like fighting the dragon outside the castle before you can take on the evil wizard inside the castle.
I know this was posted a while ago, but I‚Äôve been binging these and wanted to bring up an example I think is really interesting. You‚Äôve shown panels from the webcomic The Order of the Stick a couple of times, but the main bad guys are a pretty interesting Villain/Dragon combo. The Villain is the lich Xykon, with the goblin priest Redcloak as his Dragon. It‚Äôs established pretty quickly that Xykon has a poor memory, very little patience, and immense power. By contrast, Redcloak keeps meticulous records, focuses on buffing Xykon in combat, and has the patience of the Buddha for putting up with Xykon‚Äôs antics. But around the end of the third arc, we learn that Redcloak has been stringing Xykon along for a con so long it has spanned generations. Redcloak knows that he is not as powerful as Xykon, and is even shown genuinely scared of him on a couple of occasions. But he thinks of Xykon as a wild animal ‚Äî strong, but stupid. Easily used and easily kept on a leash ‚Äî provided you know how to handle him. He also has significantly more sympathetic motives than Xykon, and is arguably the true Big Bad of the story. Xykon does become somewhat distrustful of Redcloak briefly, but Redcloak is quickly able to compensate and work that into his plans, making contingency after contingency and ruthlessly eliminating anyone who might spill his secrets. As of the day commenting, the story isn‚Äôt finished yet, but I‚Äôm VERY excited to see how that character arc pans out.

I think Higurashi did an excellent job subverting the trend mentioned at the beginning of the video in the sixth book. At the start of the book, the arc's protagonist Rena discovers that her father is being caught in an extortion scheme by a petty mobster, and she keeps silent in an attempt to solve it herself. Quoth her directly, "Friends are nothing more than companions to share your playtime with", the implication being with such a serious situation she could only rely on herself. But when her attempt at confrontation fails, she's stuck in a situation where she's pushed to murder both the mobster and the woman who had been his front/lure. By way of her own secret keeping, her friends end up stumbling across her dismembering the bodies, and when she's forced to come clean and explain her story, the lot of them tell her that while what she did wasn't right, they still understand it, and they help her dispose of the corpses (in what was a meme-worthy sequence back in the day). The rest of the novel has her undergoing an extensive mental breakdown as a local dirty cop, a shadowy conspirator, and her own compounding paranoia drive her over the edge. When she discovers the two bodies were gone from where they were buried, as one of the others had taken them and disposed of them in a more professional spot, she nevertheless immediately thinks that person betrayed her and disappears entirely. While we're following the perspective of other protagonist Keiichi, he runs into her outside his house one night, as she tells him some of her plans and why she's going over the edge. He tries to talk her down with some platitudes about trust and not keeping secrets, until she hits him with the revelation that she knows the biggest secret he'd been keeping ever since he first showed up in the story. Much as she hates herself for it, she in the moment cracks and denounces him before leaving, him just sitting stunned in her wake. He goes through some character growth and sees how she mirrors what he did in the first novel, so when she comes back and tries to commit domestic terrorism, he gives his all to try and bring her back, by going "Fuck this conspiracy, we'll decide who's right the old fashioned way", and because he makes an over the top demand for if he wins versus giving her what she wants if she wins, she has the kinda realization that what she wants doesn't really seem worth it, so she gives up on it and asks for the reverse of his demand if she wins. So then they have a weird kinda swordfight, which she wins, and it's only when she's got him pinned and he casually tells her to kill him if she wants to win, she breaks down crying and gives up entirely, and their perspectives kinda merge as they come to the same realization of where and how things went wrong, and that "next time" they'll never make the same mistake of mistrusting each other again. And for the last two books, they don't. They (the entire main group) trust in each other, don't hide their problems, and through working together solve all the problems that had plagued them in the previous novels. It's more than a little cheesy, as Higurashi often is, but it's a compelling statement on trust and cooperation, and how much things can break down if you try to do it all yourself (which is the mistake everyone in the previous novels had made).

Spoilers for "New World":   A bit of a subversion of certain tropes. MC is an undercover cop who became an officer in the Korean mafia. He has a right hand man he cares about. When said guy is killed, MC is deeply affected. As the cops switch on a plan to have a major gang have an internal fight then sweep in and take the remaining gangsters out, the MC learns that his side (represented by a rather antagonistic cop) doesn't care about him that much and thinks of him as a disposable pawn. MC uses his knowledge to backstab the cops and becomes the new mafia overlord, all while the only people who knew he used to be an undercover cop are dead. The death of the bodyguard/right hand man was the trigger...
Spoilers for "A dirty carnival":    Features an interesting MC-bodyguard dynamic with a sad but to-be-expected betrayal. Granted, the film is one of those movies commenting on how honour codes and loyalty are disappearing from criminal organisations, and the new blood is all about backstabbing each other and always expecting to eventually be back stabbed unless they pre preemptively dispose of the betrayed first and replace them with someone indebted enough not to betray them at least in the near future.

One of my favourite Right-hand man examples is actually from Alan Becker's Animation v. Minecraft series. The RHM in question being a previously established 'reoccurring asshole/rival to close friend' towards the group of protagonists. The next time the group bumps into him is when he lures them into a trap before sneaking into their home to steal a McGuffin. It turns out his betrayal to the party is because the RHM grew up without a father, and projected that role onto the main antagonist (who tragically did not reciprocate) hoping to redeem himself in the eyes of his absent father by aiding the villains quest for vengeance. It's shown later that the antagonist is on a path of destruction because he is grieving the loss of his own son and aims to take revenge on what was responsible, never noticing how the RHM saw them. This whole series without a single line of dialog, animations of nothing more than stick figures and Minecraft of all things, its truly unexpected how deep the themes that are explored get, and how well they're represented. Grief, hatred, loyalty, desperation and forgiveness portrayed shockingly well by characters without voices or faces

So a great hand man character study is Rutabaga in BOJACK HORSEMAN. We get to see him alongside Princess Carolyn (one of the main characters and a 'good' guy) then again alongside Vanessa Gecko (Princess Carolyn's main nemesis). He plays romantic interest to PC, then neutral best friend right hand man to Gecko. Through him we get to flesh out the personality of PC, early a person looking for a change, then Gecko, a person who has the sort of life that PC wants but won't admit. He's a minor character, but seeing his relationships with both persons and how they act is a great example of writing.

Kaiba from Yu-Gi-Oh has two arguable right hand man characters - Mokuba who serves as a character foil and is in general given a lot more attention, and an actual right-hand man, Isono (or Roland in the dub), who doesn't really have much characterization except that he works for Kaiba and is clearly pretty high up in the chain of command. In my fanfics, I like to give Roland a little more depth - my favorite interpretation of him is that he serves as kind of a pseudo-father figure to Kaiba (especially in stories taking place during Kaiba's younger days) in addition to a "snarky second in command" kind of like Alfred from Batman. Their banter in those stories is fun to write, and it adds more depth to Kaiba's character in particular since, while Kaiba is prone to responding to Roland's jabs with equal amounts of sarcasm, many scenes with them actually have Kaiba taking Roland's advice (and it's understood in those stories that Roland is the sole person Kaiba would legitimately take that kind of crap from rather than brush it off).

The Boss has magic (He's only recently started learning, but he's surprisingly good at it) as well as the ability to talk in public without freaking people out (since dragons in that world generally aren't intelligent, they're animals about the size of a large dog or a big cat), and while he can usually do a pretty good job of holding his own in a fight, she sees it as her job to protect him. Doesn't stop her from feeling inadequate, low self-esteem is a pretty serious hurdle for her.  And a great source of tension.

I don't know if this has already been done, or may just be a subset of the "villain betrays dragon" thing, but this video gave me a potentially unique idea. Instead of the dragon betraying the boss because they've decided to become good, the villain is the one who betrays their dragon because they have a change of heart. Perhaps this can come about because the villain is the one witnessing the violence being done to the heroes by the dragon, and eventually has an existential crisis about it; having a "maybe I AM the villain" moment. Alternatively, maybe the villain was always moralistically complex, and either inherited their position from the previous big bad and never liked their position, was always good at heart but manipulated/brainwashed into being the big bad, or was a straight up sleeper agent the whole time and it was their plan to break down the "big bad empire" from within.

Labienus was Caesar's right hand man based on rank, knowing each other since children, and Lab. being an excellent commander.  The plan, Labienus thought, was for Caesar to become consul and give his right hand a coveted governorship.  That died when Caesar instead crossed the Rubicon and effectively declared war on the Senate.  All who followed him were denounced as traitors.  Seeing his ambition go up in smoke, Labienus switched sides and joined the Senate.  He spent the next four years being a thorn in Caesar's side.

Soundwave and Megatron
My favorite dynamic is between the Good King (or emperor for eastern culture) and the Morally Grey Chancellor or Spymaster, whereas the King will want to always do what is moral, the chancellor will often do what is necessary, much to the consternation of his superior. Usually the Chancellor will start out in opposition to the king , or at the very least be a naysayer, who gradually either comes around to the kings point of view and/or the king knows he needs the chancellor; this is especially true if the king is young and he is naive to the way of court politics. The two don‚Äôt necessarily have to like each other, but they do understand that they have to work together and present a United front to face opposition.
In retrospect, I‚Äôm surprised you didn‚Äôt use Soundwave to highlight the difference between the RHM and the Starscream.  Because while Soundwave is never Megatron‚Äôs second in command, he is ABSOLUTELY Megatron‚Äôs RHM.  He‚Äôs the Chief Communication Officer for a reason, he monitors EVERYTHING aboard the ship.  What‚Äôs more he‚Äôs a survivor of the same gladiatorial backstory as Megatron, and it SHOWS every time Soundwave fights.  I‚Äôm convinced that Megatron wouldn‚Äôt be nearly as patient with Starscream‚Äôs BS if he didn‚Äôt have Soundwave to keep tabs on him and apply pressure.

My all time favorite Right Hand Man is Wesley from Daredevil Season 1.  Fisk is a major crime lord, but is awkward and generally soft spoken.  Wesley is the fixer, the translator, and the guy Fisk sends to talk to other bosses or enlist the help of blackmail targets, etc.  The best part is their friendship, however.  When Fisk becomes attached to Vanessa partway through Season 1, Wesley is actually supportive.  While Wesley personally considers New York disgusting, he sees Fisk's love for the city and helps him try to change it.  When the heroes get close to someone who Fisk loves, Wesley tries to take care of it on his own because Fisk is currently under a lot of stress.  And it's not just a one way street, because  Fisk truly considers Wesley as a friend.  After Wesley is gone, Fisk makes it clear that other people who fill Wesley's role aren't replacing Wesley. Wesley is just such a good character for other reasons, and his acting is perfect.

To explain the "dragon" title. It's a generic fantasy tableop term used because often an evil wizard's  "Dragon" is an actual dragon they somehow leashed to their will, usually through mind control. This dynamic also sets up other tropes like the Xanathos Gambit where defeating the BBEG will unleash the Dragon, now free of control, to exact revenge on the humans who used it as a slave.

A trope I would like to see you discuss is that of the famous/infamous "Not like this" moments. I propose two examples, one I feel is done right and one done poorly. In The Last Jedi we see one character stop another from sacrificing themselves, were upon they give a speech about how this isn't the way to accomplish what they want. While this might feel like a good sentiment it feels like it just... flops. On the other end of the spectrum, the moment in Full Metal Alchemist Brotherhood when Colonel Mustang finally has the chance to get revenge for his best friends death but, in that moment, we have characters pointing out how what he is doing and how he is doing it isn't okay (I feel it should be noted he literally has his boot on the persons metaphorical neck at this point) and he instead backs down. On paper these moments are identical, but there is something about the execution of them that completely sets them apart. I'd like to know why, but haven't really been able to pinpoint a solid reason. Is it the framing? The scene itself? The voice acting/acting? Or is it the story building up to that moment that makes it matter?

While I'm largely ambivalent about the show as a whole, Star Wars: The Clone Wars did an excellent job of building up this trope.  Throughout the show, the relationship between Jedi and the individual Clone Troopers under their command is built up and explored.  Captain Rex is 100% Anakin's right-hand man, and Ahsoka's too before she leaves the Jedi.  Time and time again, the Jedi encourage the clones' individuality (most of them, anyway), and in return are granted complete loyalty....up until Order 66.  And then it all goes out the window.  Bodyguard betrayal on a galactic scale.  With one main exception: Rex.  Rex remains loyal to who Anakin *was* instead of who he *becomes* and manages to get out of following the order with Ahsoka's help.

A interesting rework of the lieutenant/captain ive seen is the General/ADC (aide de camp) which also doesnt limit the "right hand" to one person. Yes its almost an exclusive to military fiction but it has interesting implications. this iteration also usually involves the ADC dying unfortunately. The biggest example i can think is from the sharpe series, where the POV character as an officer, routinely finds his juniors getting themselves killed trying to imitate him (regardless of how hard he tries to keep them out of harms way, due to the setting nature that these characters have a very real chance of being literal children). it happens enough that the author calls it out as his "curse" at one point, and he requests not to be given any more junior officers, as they keep dying, and the only one who does survive the whole saga is a chronic drunk with no delusions of imitating his boss.

One of the most important types of right hand man is the "Enforcer" one right hand man. The character that in the absence of his boss, serves as his representative and enforces his will where and on who need be, making sure things run swimmingly. This kind of character can sometime become the face or the the main associated being to an organization. The best examples of this kind of right hand man are Darth Vader and Azula, who each became the symbol of the evil entity which they were serving.

The reason the Dragon is called the Dragon is because the Dragon usually represents the immediate physical threat.  Imagine the Dragon rampaging in the Hero's hometown as the Big Bad broods ominously in his Spooky Tower.  For example, in Star Wars, Vader is the biggest direct threat to the protagonists for most of the original trilogy.  In Willow, Sorsha is a much more direct threat before her Heel-Face Turn.  In the typical Arthur-Mordred-Morgana story with Arthur as the good-guy, Morgana is the Big Bad in the background as Mordred is the immediate threat to Arthur.  The Witch King of Angmar is a bigger threat to Frodo and the good guys while Sauron sits in his tower and is a spooky presence in the background.    Per TV Tropes:  The Dragon drifted over time and is now the right hand of the Big Bad, sometimes being the Evil Counterpart of the heroic Number Two as well. It was originally intended to mean something like a "penultimate threat," or an antagonist that's a more physical challenge for the protagonist before they face the Big Bad as a mental challenge. The redefining reflects the trope's visibility and the various sub-tropes that regard The Dragon as evil's #2.

there is also an interesting dynamic with the smart guy when he's the dragon instead of the BBEG. Typically he is the capable brains of the operation but he also tends to have some character flaw which limits his driving factor. Such as being squeamish or too detail-obsessed to move towards a goal while their BBEG is typically the overly ambitious type.
A really good example of a master-servant, Brain-Brawn, and bodyguard-principal relationship is that of Artemis Fowl and Butler.  (The book version, not the failed adaptation.)  Beyond Butler's steadfast loyalty to the family and his charge, and Artemis fully respecting what Butler does for him in furthering his plans, their dynamic evolves to a surrogate father-son relationship.  Their mutually loyalty and respect persists even after Butler is forced to retire after nearly dying too many times for Artemis and getting too old to efficiently do his job.
I always thought of the reason the villains right hand man was called "The Dragon" was based in the types of stories where the villain isn't a physical threat if you can actually get into a fight with them, the evil wizard mind controlling a dragon type thing, and that's why the dragon can be so quick to betray them or end up becoming a bigger villain after the boss is taken care of, because now they're an ANGRY dragon. Basically I always took this one as being named based on a literal older used version of the trope. tldr, don't let evil wizards mind control dragons.
I think the villain's Right Hand Man is called The Dragon because it may have started with them being the villain's attack dog, and if the villain themselves isn't a dragon their loyal overpowered servant could be a dragon.  I think this reasoning is a bit outdated, as for some time dragons have not been exclusively evil narratively and often are just apex hyper-predators rather than evil.  (Sort of how cats would look at mice.)  Dragons are often "bonded" by some way to a master or rider, this may also have contributed. An interesting reason why the Dragon mite betray the Villain may be because the Dragon's loyalty was to the cause and not the Villain.  If the Villain got too egotistical and megalomaniacle putting the execution of the cause at risk, then the cause may need a new leader.  I think we've seen this in real history also, where the cause or office garnered more loyalty than who occupied it. And Azula being "thrown off her game" is such a subtle understatement.

I think the title "The Dragon" may have come from media where a literal dragon was a big enemy, but not the villain of the piece. While it's more common for villains to have a toxic relationship / friendship with their sidekicks, i also like instances where a hero / side character who is mostly good still has an unhealthy, toxic or even abusive relationship with their right-hand man; since it shows that anyone can be toxic, not just a villain.   Examples of this are Rose and Pearl from SU (this is much less abusive and more of an unhealthy obsession from Pearl), Varrick and Zhu Li from LoK (Varrick was positively abusive to Zhu Li and treated her as a tool), Chase Deveneaux and Julia Argent from Carmen Sandiego (Chase isn't a full-blown abuser and definitely has good intentions, but he still bullied Julia a lot and made light of her skills).

My theory on why TVTropes calls it "the Dragon" is because in fantasy dragons are traditionally dangerous and powerful threats to the heroes, but not THE biggest bad out there (for example, in Lord of the Rings, Smaug was the one the dwarves wanted to take down, but Gandalf was worried about the more dangerous, far-off evil that was Sauron). That being said, I'm not sure how they got to the conclusion of making the Dragon apply to right-hand men, since dragons tend to be solitary figures.

5:43 I checked the current "The Dragon" page on TV Tropes, and I couldn't find it, but I recall (which could be wrong, I might need to check the Trope's history page...) a mention of the etymology of the trope being that in the original _Dragon Quest_ the evil wizard Big Bad has a _literal_ dragon that works as his direct subordinate and is one of the two final bosses along with the wizard himself, and that might be the origin of the term. EDIT: I found it, and I was too specific in the example, but correct on the idea. The reason the trope has the name it does is simply because in a _lot_ of fiction, the hero has to fight monsters, often dragons, to get to the villain who's behind all the evil and usually has something to do with the monsters, just like how a villainous Dragon is usually the one the hero has to take down first in order to get to the Big Bad in stories.

hey, i actually accidentally flipped the ‚Äúrhm cares for the villain but the villain doesn‚Äôt care about them‚Äù dynamic completely on its head, and i didn‚Äôt even notice! in one of my stories, the main ‚Äúbad guy‚Äù duo of the comic are a lady and her general, who also happens to be her son. in her own way, the lady actually cares about the general, and while he does serve as an extension of her will in politics and the battlefield, it‚Äôs kind of obvious she also harbours some motherly love for him - there is a scene i have drafted that reminds me of mufasa showing simba all of their kingdom between them, but. evil? i guess?  the whole deal between the lady and the general is that it *seems* like a mutually familial (though pretty dysfunctional) relationship, with the both of them having the same goal, but as the story goes on, it becomes increasingly more and more clear that while the lady cares and trusts the general with her life, the general frankly does not give a single shit and would rather do everything himself, but needs support from her for now because of how she‚Äôs established herself politically. it‚Äôs not a gradual hatred like suggested, in fact i‚Äôm planning the foreshadowing out so that if you look back and re-read the comic it‚Äôs pretty obvious that the general has his own wants and never cared in the first place. this comes in at the 3rd act breakdown, where the general breaks through a curse that was put upon him to weaken him down and becomes the much more terrifying and formidable alternative villain, who ends up fully betraying the lady and killing her. it‚Äôs interesting to me that the only thing i needed to flip this dynamic is adding a partial dependence of the right hand man to their boss, but only up until certain conditions are met, and it wasn‚Äôt even that bad of a work environment! come on, general, at least your mother had SOME ethics!

where else is she gonna go?  so suddenly she's effectively on her own, having been abandoned by the only person she believes cares about her and on some level, she knows he doesn't. this is what finally pushes her over the edge‚Äì she can no longer fully trust the person she devoted herself to, so how can she trust anyone? One of my favorite Right Hand Man dynamics is when removing the hand only succeeds in making things far worse. Situations like the right hand acting as a moral anchor or power limiter for the boss and by killing them. For example when Frieza killed Krillin to spite Goku it caused Goku to become enraged enough to go super sayan and beat Frieza into paste (ignoring the fact that Krillin was FAR weaker than Goku he still acted as Goku's right hand by virtue of being his best friend). I do like this concept most though when the villain is actually given a foreshadowing of a redemption arc by showing a positive and caring bond between them and their dragon, only to have any hope of that arc scattered to the wind by the heroes taking the dragon out and making an otherwise affable antagonist go full genocidal villain due to no longer having any reason to care anymore.
I love how the right hands of the Magic casters are coming in with fire extinguishers ready.
An interesting twist on this trope is in one of my favorite Game Series- Overlord. You play as the evil overlord, going out and conquering the land, but your right hand man in the games is Gnarl. The main feature of the games is the minions, basically evil pikmin, and Gnarl is the oldest and most inteligent of them, the one that leads the group when they are between Overlords. The dynamic is a cross between the dragon and villian, and leftenate and Captain, built on a foundation of an inversion of the brain and brawn. Gnarl acts as the tutorial and guide Npc, like if Navi was an evil granpa goblin, so while he is your subordinate he is the one with the ideas and plots, helped along by the fact that like the link/navi pairing, the overlord is always a silent protagonist.  Is weird but fun, because despite being in the perfect position for an evil Vizier type scheme or two, Gnarl shows no signs of disloyalty, other than that he is loyal to the POSITION and not the person. Considering how you create minions throughout the games, how slavishly loyal all the other minions are, and how things tend to go down hill between overlords (necesitating the player go and gather up the minion hives and other objects to  enable their dark reign each game) Gnarl may not be able to rebel or backstab. The minions have loyalty baked into them from birth, Gnarl's experience and intellect just means that loyalty leads to more administrative and intellectual support. Going back to the Navi comparison I always enjoyed his guidance and dialogue, because while it did just lead you straight through the game, it was full of character and charm, and just differential enough to not feel like you were being bossed around, like a friend pointing out something while watching you play a game.  Another fun example is the pair of right hand men in the Downy Holmes movies. Watson is Holmes' right hand man, and the first film does good work showing why, and how they bounce off of each other, with the second doubling down on it and introducing Moriarty and Moran, as dark reflections of the heroes. Moriarty is well known but Moran less so, in the books he was an assassin, introduced in the Return of Sherlock Holmes, trying to kill Holmes in revenge for Moriarty's death, in the films he is still an assassin but also the Dragon going out and doing the more active legwork for Moriarty. Moriarty isn't as hands on as Holmes but is far from helpless, but Moran is the one who goes running after the heroes to shoot them dead, and the one going off to deal with loose ends and such while Moriarty mostly distances himself from things when possible. Both relationships are fun, but I find the implied history facinating. Holmes and Watson have certainly had plenty of adventures, but how did the relationship start isn't explored, both because we have the books and because the current change, Watson getting married and planning to leave the duo, is more interesting and more pertinent to explore. Moran and Moriarty have an even more mysterious relationship, because while Moriarty doesn't shy away from cutting loose ends, Moran is the one who does so, amongst other duties that imply high amounts of trust, making the origins of their partnership, and the motivations behind it, a fun unexplored story.

Great video!
My favorite two examples of this trope are in A Practical Guide to evil. Scribe and Adjutant were two of my favorite characters and the series did a really good job of making them well rounded characters with their own priorities which doesn't always happen for with this trope.
There's also a scenario where the Villain ends up faltering in their plans, by having a change of heart or from realizing that they may be going too far and things are getting a little out of hand, leading the Dragon to backstab the Villain because "they are weak", but the Villain survives and escapes.   This can end up a couple of different ways. First, with the good guys now having to deal with TWO villains, with the Dragon having been smart enough to scheme his way into power and the now wiser-and-angrier Villain making a comeback to both punish/kill their treacherous former underling and deal with the annoying-and-no-longer-amusing good guys. Second, with the Villain potentially teaming up with the Hero/Heroes to take down the former Dragon-turned-Bigger-Bad, with the former Villain possibly pulling a complete "Redemption-Arc" flip and joining the good guys for real, as the token Dark Teammate or otherwise.  Another possible scenario is the reverse version of the 'Bodyguard Crush', where the Villain cares about the Dragon, but the Dragon doesn't return those feelings, either for reasons of professionalism or because the Dragon has...other plans. The Dragon 'pretending' to reciprocate the Villains feelings until they choose to drop the act is also an option. Two end results of this come to mind. One: the Villains' will breaks and they fall apart, diving headlong into a Villain Redemption Scene/Arc when they realize that none of their former motivations matter any more and helping the good guys becomes the right/only thing left to do. Two: the Villains' mind breaks and they completely loose their shit, because if their life doesn't have any more meaning, why should anything else? This can quickly spiral out of hand, with the good guys going from dealing with a difficult-but-manageable villain to now facing a completely unhinged "end-game final-form mega-boss" villain with nothing left to lose and no issues whatsoever with destroying everyone and everything in a "FEEL MY PAIN!!!" doomsday plot.  A grief-driven-earth-shattering-kaboom like this can also happen in a Bad End version of a Bodyguard Sacrifice where the Villain and Dragon genuinely care for each other and one of them gets hurt or killed, leading the other to disproportionally retaliate in a potentially-world-breaking fashion. If the hurt/dead member of the pair is healed or brought back, this can turn into a Redemption Arc, leading the evil duo to decide that having their partner is worth more than being evil. If the hurt/dead person is gone for good with no way of reversing this, then the Armageddon clock starts ticking. Sometimes, this can be resolved with the heroes talking down the bad guy in a "Is this what they would have wanted?" friendship speech, which itself can potentially lead into a Redemption Arc. Other times, "Suicide-by-Hero" is the only acceptable option to the Villain, with the Villain falling in a "tragic and emotional death scene" and the sympathetic Heroes morning the Villains' death and re-evaluating their personal motivations in their own character-building story arcs.  Interestingly enough, this can also happen to a heroic character, where something bad happens to a loved one and the hero breaks entirely, loosing all sense of morality or restraint and completely replacing/surpassing the original Villain as the new Big-Bad. The entire storyline of the Injustice League is a good example of this. =^x^=

9:38 One REALLY interesting 'reversal' of the whole 'Bodyguard Betrayal' trope I've seen is when its the once-heroic BOSS who's spiraled into a full-blown villain-turn, but is also dragging the still-very-heroic RHM along and still expecting their unquestioning loyalty. Which of course leaves the RHM in an EXTREMELY awkward position of choosing between their loyalty and what they know is right. Either way this goes can be give some intense character drama and very spicy twists. Maybe the RHM takes the 'My Master Right or Wrong' approach and our heroes are forced to fight a former friend who they KNOW is an entirely good person for lots of tragedy points. Or the RHM does choose morals over loyalty and turns on their boss, which among other things can lead to a VERY tragic showdown between these two once-close comrades.

In my story, I have a right hand (wo)man who is the brains while the hero is the brawn.  The woman starts out with strong feelings for the hero and her motivation is to always be reliable to him. I think the closest comparison would be the "Captain/Lieutenant" scenario since she basically runs the rest of the "crew" and it's kind of a set hierarchy. Over time though, she realizes that her feelings will never be reciprocated by the hero because he just doesn't see her that way. At first she is heartbroken but then she realizes that even though he doesn't see her romantically, he still values her and depends on her, and for her that's enough, and she is able to let go of those romantic feelings and (potentially) fall in love with someone else while still loyally serving as the hero's right hand woman.

A variation on the Villain & Dragon dysfunctional relationship trope I've seen more recently is when the Dragon is genuinely loyal to the Villain and idealises them, which gets twisted when the Villain undergoes a Face Turn and the Dragon starts fighting to stop it.  It's a fun one because the Dragon is fighting to maintain the Villain's power but becomes the driving the threat in the story, undermining themselves. It can also add a lot of juicy nuance to a Face Turn as the Villain is forced to face the consequences of their past Villainous behaviour on which the loyalty was built on, especially if they weren't always a great boss.

I think another Right Hand Man trope that is probably easy to lump in with Bodyguard to Badass is the Student to Mentor trope, where the Student who typically has some cool ability is being trained by a Mentor who makes the Student do all the work and only intervenes if badassery is necessary. This is often more of a temporary trope wherein a character is thrust into a duo as a part of their training, but it's still important since it has a similar authority dynamic to the Right Hand Man trope and it's seen in most training arcs, some school settings, and a lot of anime. Think of Mob and Reigen from Mob Psycho 100, Wing and Zushi from Hunter x Hunter, Yuji and Todo and Nanami and Ino from Jujutsu Kaisen, and Dredd and Cassandra Anderson from Dredd (2013) and Daniel LaRusso and Mr. Miyagi from The Karate Kid (1984) for Western examples.

I think the Villain and Dragon trope name comes from an old D&D/ high fantasy final boss trend. IE: final boss is a mindflayer or a magic undead and their lair is protected by a dragon.
This one's a bit unconventional, considering it comes from a Minecraft fan novel, but I think my favorite example of Dragon Redemption is from the Chronicles of Elementia. Leonidas, the dragon in this example, is one of many to the villain, Caesar, but Leo goes through his own character arc. By the second book, the reader's spent a lot of time with him, and they know his internal thoughts and his hesitancy to commit the atrocities he has. By the end of the second book, during the major battle that sees a lot of main characters dead/captured, Leonidas confronts Caesar in combat, fully catching the latter by surprise. Caesar, being a general, has seen very little combat, but still knows how to defend himself, while Leonidas has been fighting his whole life. It doesn't take long for Caeser to make a mistake and for Leo to put a few arrows in his head. From then on, Leonidas is a fugitive, all because he wanted out of Caesar's control and revenge for his family

So, one reference I think would enrich this analysis a bit would be: Neon Genesys Evangelion. Shinji, I think (and, to some extent, all EVA pilots and most characters in the story) work for most of the story as Shinji's father's henchmen. It's even more interesting because, usually, right hand men who get away switch sides or just don't come back. As they aren't the main characters, their stories are often shortened and their switch happens in the end, or the most dramatic moment, while Shinji, like the depressed person he is, refuses, then accepts the call, runs from home, comes back, and takes his damn time making up his mind.

It probably dips into other tropes but there's also the...hidden/true dragon trope, in which the right hand man is the real big bad. Whether they manipulate or mind control or whatever, the right hand man is revealed to have been behind it all. The hand behind the curtain type deal which ties in with the dragon betrayal trope but is just a little deeper since it makes the original villain actually a victim all along.   This tends to end with some grand reveal where once the villain dies, by their hand or otherwise, the secret villain reveals themselves to have been the end boss.   The best scenario I can think of is the heroes get a good dose of guilt when they find out they killed the wrong guy, and now need to fight a more nefarious villain who at the least shows a greater degree of depravity and at least a mind trickery skill set whether through social manipulation or straight up mind powers.
There's another twist that would be worth mentioning: when the loyalty of the right-hand mand to the boss comes from the shared goals, and the boss is the one that starts faltering, which leads to the betrayal by RHM. It's a unique situation, that it is a betrayal that may be resolved peacefully, when the boss recognizes his error and goes back on the right path. Example: killing Envy in FMA.

I really love the way One Piece approaches this trope, especially with the Straw Hats. While most people argue that Zoro is Luffy's right hand man, other Straw Hats qualify for this position. When it comes to leadership, planning and strategy, Luffy's not the smartest guy around (and he's often not around) so you'll find other Straw Hats stepping up to take lead of the group. The structure of the crew doesn't solely rely on the presence/absence of one character.   Strength-wise, Luffy is by far the strongest in the crew. However, Zoro and Sanji are both high contenders for the position of right hand man. Because of that, the dynamic is structured in a way that both individuals are necessary to carry Luffy forward i.e. wings of the Pirate King. Funny enough, most of the other crews have a well defined structure and hierarchy but the Straw Hats are quite unique in that dynamic.

To quote the article on relevant tropes from Tropedia   The term "dragon" actually originates from folklore where the hero will often end up fighting an actual dragon before even fighting the more intelligent but weaker Big Bad and described as such in "The Hero Of A Thousand Faces", a non-fiction comparison of various fantasy heroes written by Joseph Campbell. Hence, dragons (or stand-ins for them) are often portrayed as a Big Bad's second-in-command.
Here's the problem I've always had with this trope: If the Dragon is more badass than the Big Bad and is more involved in the plot and action with taking down the heroes, why is the Dragon relegated to second in command? Shouldn't he be the #1 threat since the Big Bad is more background most of the time? The Dragon makes more sense as the main antagonist than the guy just sitting on his throne for 95% of the story.

I'd like to highlight the Rykard/Tanith relationship in Elden Ring, it's definitely similar though far out of bounds from the trope. Rykard is the lord and possesses all of the strength, whereas Tanith (who is also his consort) is the only one who stood by his side when he turned to such a horrific form of blasphemy. Tanith is only ever seen two places, one where she is sitting on a chair in front of a painting of Rykard with a giant knight to her side, and one much later on. She does not fight at all, but she definitely is Rykard's right hand man, directly numerous champions to Rykard so he can devour them and gain strength.  She is not a bodyguard, but a manipulator for Rykard. And when Rykard eventually falls, she moves to where he was and is seen eating his head, saying that he'll live on inside of her and that they will devour the gods together. Also notable is that since you are the one killing Rykard, it's unusual that even after the fight she refers to him as 'our lord' and doesn't resent the Main Character for slaying him, merely stating he will get stronger, presumably from inside of her.    I just thought this was such a unique turn on this trope while still humanizing both Tanith and Rykard without them ever directly interacting once.

My favorite variant of this trope is ‚ÄúThe Secretary‚Äù. While the boss is the one doing all the cool flashy big picture stuff, the Secretary is the one working behind the scenes, handling the logistics of the boss‚Äô plans and cleaning up any messes the boss may have left behind both metaphorically and literally. These ones are great to explore because they clearly have a much higher bullshit tolerance than most and it‚Äôs interesting to find out why these people are working themselves to the bone just to ensure the boss can go forward with their plan unimpeded. It‚Äôs especially fun when it turns out these guys who we thought were purely desk workers are actually incredibly capable in a fight but only resort to that when all other options are exhausted. Also they are always a good set up for any workaholism jokes.  Examples: Ms. Pauline from Team Fortress 2, Owen from Gargoyles and Smithers from The Simpsons.

One example of this trope I also really like is the relationship between Edelgard and Hubert in Fire Emblem Three Houses. Hubert is the right-hand man and it's actually his family's job to be right-hand men to Edelgard's family, but his loyalty to Edelgard comes only from his affection for her and his belief in Edelgard's goals, so much that he actually kills his own father for having betrayed Edelgard's father and Edelgard herself by proxy. But since Edelgard is a morally gray figure, having good intentions but using violent means to reach her goals, her relationship to Hubert is also a mix of hero/villain type of dynamic with the right-hand man because Edelgard acts like a hero boss while Hubert fully embraces the villain right-hand man archetype, despite acknowledging that Edelgard is (and HAS to be) a hero-like figure, which leads to Edelgard wanting Hubert to also find love and happiness and to share his burdens while Hubert does all the horrible things that have to be done on his own, sometimes without Edelgard's knowledge, and with a smile on his face because he is convinced that Edelgard has to stay a hero for her dream to be fulfilled and by doing all those things for her on his own, by bloodying his own hands without letting a drop touch her, he's doing his part, the one he chose for himself, in making her dreams come true. Obviously heroic duos and villainous duos are a lot more common than morally gray duos, but Edelgard and Hubert's relationship is one of my favourite iterations of this trope because of how unique it is

The Sith in Star Wars is just one long chain of Dragons killing their boss to be the boss. What's even betternis the Sith masters not only expect this betrayal, but even encourage it. Once the apprentice learns enough from their mentors, they kill them or die trying. The apprentice succeeding just means they've outgrown their master and now become the master, ensuring each new Sith is stronger than the last. The apprentice failing just means the master has to try again with another apprentice, usually one more loyal than the last so the betrayal doesn't come too early.
I think the "spiciest" part of the Villainous Right-Hand Man trope is that we never know how it's going to end (unless the backstabbing is made blatantly obvious from the start). And even if they're villains, if we like them, we WANT for their relationship - platonic or otherwise - to work out. And that uncertainty makes us care even more.

Order of the Stick has an interesting variation of this with its main villain pair, Xykon the lich and Redcloak, his right-hand-goblin. In the main story, Xykon's magic makes him absurdly powerful, but Redcloak is a dangerous mage in his own right, and more than one character notices that Redcloak's subservience doesn't seem to make sense given how little he gets from Xykon in return. In a prequel story, we get to see how they initially met. Redcloak had a villainous plan he couldn't complete on his own, Xykon happened to have the abilities that Redcloak needed and lacked, and they formed an alliance of convenience. However, as things go awry and the plan ends up costing both of them far more than they bargained for, Redcloak adopts a serious sunk-costs complex, convincing himself that not only will it all be worth it when the plan comes to fruition, but that seeing it through to the end is the only way he can live with what he's done. Xykon, realizing that Redcloak can't stop as long there's even a sliver of a chance that he can pull it off, manipulates Redcloak into committing even more heinous actions in service of the plan. Then Xykon outright tells Redcloak that now they both know Redcloak will do anything, no matter how much he hates it, to keep Xykon from abandoning the plan, which gives Xykon all the power in their relationship since he can walk away from the plan whenever he likes. Redcloak is stuck catering to Xykon's whims, not out of any loyalty to Xykon himself, but because the only alternative is acknowledging that everything he already did was for nothing.

I had the boss loving the right hand man thing (though it was familiial rathern than romantic) thing in one of my rpg campaigns. The main villian was a wizard who had adopted a troubled young street urchin with a natural talent (one would say need) for violence. The wizard, while a well intentioned extremist type of villain, didn't have the stomach for the less savoury acts he knew would be needed for his plans, and thus, thought this kid would suit his purposes. Over time, they came to genuinely care for each other like father and son, to the point that the wizard would teleport his right hand man away from danger if he started taking too much damage. He would try to wave it off as "I have more use for him", but the PCs who saw this soon cottoned on to the truth... and saw it as a chance to redeem them both. One time, the PCs even helped the right hand man rescue his boss from another enemy, reasoning that as psychotic as this young man was, with the wizard, he at least had focus, and wouldn't just be killing randomly.

The Stormlight Archives had a really interesting version of the Right-Hand Man (at least through book 4; spoilers that I won't go into here).  In SA, Kaladin is the big physical threat. He's an absurdly gifted fighter with a spear as a regular person, but then he gets the magic powers and is even more deadly. His right hand man, Teft, meanwhile, is a competent fighter and Sergeant, but nothing super amazing. What Teft has that Kaladin lacks is knowledge about what Kaladin's abilities should be. It could be argued that Kaladin has other Right-Hand Men - Moash and Rock both spring to mind - but Teft is the most literal definition for it as he's the one that Kaladin turns to in terms of the direct chain of command.
I'm quite partial to the dragon betrayal that amounts to "for your own good". Like the villian is going to go too far and hurt themselves, or the dragon has realised that their goal is pointless so betrays them to save them.
The dragon comes from Jung.  I'd say lieutenant/adjutant is an arguably more realistic pairing than captain/commander. A lieutenant is usually educated but inexperienced, a new officer fresh out of the academy. Their adjutant is a veteran sergeant who's technically lower rank, training and advising the lieutenant with the benefit of experience.

While my favourite Villain/Dragon pair is probably Xehanort/Xigbar from Kingdom Hearts, primarily because of the latter character and his personal reasoning for being there, I must admit that probably the best Villain/Dragon dynamic is that of Xemnas and Sa√Øx from the same series. On his initial appearance their goals are shared, or so it would seem- they both lack a heart and want to get them back, not only to feel emotion other than longing, but in Sa√Øx's case, because he's lead to believe that if he dies without a heart, that's the end of him. He dies woeful that he fell just before Kingdom Hearts is completed, meaning he was so close to his goal, yet so far. Then he wakes up. Feeling feelings. And Xemnas says "Hey so you signed up for this big thing we're doing, come along now." Sa√Øx's foremost feeling is regret for what he did to some of the younger heroes, and he realises- he did those things out of jealousy. He *did* have emotions. He *had* a heart, just a tainted one. He was being used the entire time, but his hands are tied and he can't go back. However, he has some new knowledge that benefits his possibility of betrayal - the knowledge that death is bullshit! He sets out a plan to bring back the fallen heroes and return them to their mutual friend, and knows that he has to die before the job can be finished, but he's been through that pain once, and can now do so again with the solace of knowing he did the right thing. In the end, he reconciles with the heroes he hurt and gets his happily ever after. Unless Kingdom Hearts 4+ has anything to say about it.

I really like the Undervalued Underling trope when its done on the hero side of the story. Which I /mostly/ see in fic (looking at you voltron fic writers and also thank you üëè), in books where the underling is, surprise! actually the main character, or in sequel (or prequel) books/series about the underling. But the reason I like them is because it shows the main character has flaws in very real and tangible ways for many of the audience to relate to in one direction or the other. Many people have undervalued or have been undervalued in their life, and especially with very loyal relationships it can leave a deep impression on people that makes them like certain characters who go through or do these things more.

3:45 I just wanna point out that interestingly in many chinese media the right hand man is often more a counselor or officer of some sort. A man able to coordinate or give advices. To hint at things so the main realises things or learn more. Or simply to make the power house main character reach full potential. Taking a bit of a teacher role.  Else simply allowing the boss to go and be the action man while the right hand man covers the flank and holds the fort. Or in cases, solving problems before they become a problem. Its also an easy way to write off all the work needed to keep an army and letting the main character advance the plot.  one of my favorite quotes is : An assasin might spend weeks of concentration, and months of preparation for the perfect assasination. But a counselor will always kill more then assasins can in their lifetimes. They can plot the deaths of 12 people in no time and ultimately choses their demise.

ONE PIECE SPOILERS Now I finally have a term for Zoro from One Piece, since he doesn't really act as a lancer to Luffy. He stands out by being stoic and serious in contrast to his captain who is goofy and lighthearted; another detail is he is absolutley loyal with no hesitation after joining the crew, even being the one to take the tough decisions for the sake of helping the group, most notably his stance after Ussop's defection. Sanji also brings an interesting betrayal twist not mentioned in the video: Reluctant Betrayal, in wich the RHM has to abandon or even go up against the boss due to their life or a love one being in danger and having no other choice but to join the bad guys. Sanji is forced into an arranged wedding or else his adoptive father gets killed, and in order to save both him and the rest of the crew, he pushes them away and even fights Luffy. This betrayal either ends in a tragic death or, in Sanji's case, a rescue mission.

I've always been a fan of the idea of the bad guy and dragon being in love, or other such relationship, and if one dies than it triggers a grief induced powerup for the survivor that makes them WAAAAAAAAAY more dangerous than they were previously.  One of my favorite examples is an old ps2 games called shadow hearts: from the new world, where there's a villain/sidekick duo named Lady and killer (ya, not very original, I know). Lady is literally an emotionless husk of her former self, but she brought killer back from near death and so he feels obligated to defend her from they party because she saved his life. At the end of the game, he dies to the party in a way that she can't revive him (seemingly at the time) and she flies off the handle in an emotion fueled rage, the only emotion she'd showed through the entire game. It was pretty sad, and you genuinely feel sorry for her at that moment.

Regarding Walter, the manga goes into why he betrayed the Hellsing organization. To sum it up: existential dread. He saw Alucard, pinnacle of vampires and forever "young" as long as he wished it, if he didn't wish to be a girl instead because he did do that at times. He and Alucard fought alongside each other during World War 2 when Walter was at his prime. However, mortals age. Over time Walter saw his capabilities decreasing as his body withered, unable to fight time itself. And at the same times Walter saw Alucard the same as he'd ever been from the time they first met, unchanged and still at his peak as he would always be. And Walter was jealous of that immutability, he hated having to get weaker over time just because he was a human. He didn't even have the option of asking Alucard to make him a vampire, mostly because Alucard would have absolutely refused but also because Walter was male and a vampire could only be created from a virgin of the opposite sex to the vampire. Also you absolutely know Walter fucks so that was out the window. In any case, he wanted that gift of immortal stasis Alucard had and well, you know the rest. Ironically Alucard was ALSO jealous of Walter for the opposite reasons, because in his eyes to live and die as a human being was a privilege he would never be afforded no matter how many years passed. This is why he venerates the elderly and considers them to be truly beautiful.
