One villain - dragon relationship I find interesting is Ch'rell and Karai in the 2003 TMNT series. It is an example of a "father daughter" relationship as Ch'rell took a young Karai in and raised her. Even trained her in martial arts. What makes it interesting is that Ch'rell is a full on villain. Only caring for his own personal power and revenge on his fellow Utroms. And yet he took Karai in and raised her. Now one can say that he only did it to gain an ally by gaslighting her from a young age. But why do that at all? Ch'rell even leaves Karai back in Japan to lead the Foot clan there while he goes to New York. Karai even knows that Ch'rell is an Utrom before the reveal to the rest of the cast. Not even Ch'rells former right hand, Hun knew about that (and when Hun does learn the truth, he losses any respect he had for "The Shredder"). Another important moment comes when Karai is captured by Agent Bishop and is used as a bargaining tool. Ch'rell orders Hun (whom is on thin ice) to rescue Karai. If Ch'rell only saw her as a tool, why risk sending in "dead weight" to rescue her? Ch'rell, on some level has to care for his adopted daughter. Even if he doesn't show it most of the time.

My favorite pairing of this is Senku/Taiju from Dr. Stone, because the "Right hand man might be their side piece" bit is subverted right away, with Taiju having his own ladyfriend he's fiercely devoted to, and Senku being.... well... for lack of a gentler way to put this, about as asexual as they come. While Red's focusing on action-adventure, RHMs show up everywhere, and come in all sorts of flavors.  A very common RHM function is to be the grounding force that keeps their boss on the rails. Either by direct action, or by serving as their voice of reason/moral guide post, often both. In many cases, that may actually be their primarcy role.  Alfred and Jeeves are both very grounded, capable men who work for somebody who're one short step from a spectacular collapse.  Also fun, it's quite common for them to also fall into at least one other classic pair trope.

The very first arc of Naruto had a very interesting example of this trope! I don‚Äôt suppose I need to warn for spoilers, since the series is like two decades old.   Basically, the villain and the dragon have an unrequited loyalty dynamic, where the dragon is much younger than their boss, and is willing to do anything for him. The dragon doesn‚Äôt even like fighting people, let alone killing, but they value their boss too much to protest. The dragon ends up dying when they throw them self in the way of an attack meant for their boss. Our protagonist cries about how terrible it is that the dragon threw their life away for someone who meant the world to them, but only saw them as a tool. Boss proceeds to SOB over dead dragon, who he secretly cared about all along, and then dies from injuries sustained from murdering HIS OWN boss, who had kicked the dragon‚Äôs body. The dragon died believing that the only person they cared about didn‚Äôt feel the same, and their boss died apologizing to them.  Naruto was like 700 episodes long but the antagonists from the very first arc were still incredibly memorable.

Not to drag every single conversation about media back to Our Flag Means Death, but I think the relationship between Ed and his right hand man (of sorts) izzy is super interesting here. I saw someone else in the comments talk about the trope where the villain has a mini redemption arc and then gets betrayed by their right hand who's just far more committed to the whole "being evil" thing than they were, and izzy is such a fascinating example of that kind of dynamic. He has this idolised fixation on the legend of blackbeard, and takes such an active role in forcing him not to change or heal emotionally or accept love because he sees this as a perversion of the essence of blackbeard - which makes for such compelling conflict, especially since by the end of the first season he kind of wins. The fact that he's so devoted to this ideal to the extent that being threatened with death, even getting his toe cut off and being forced to eat it, is his victory scenario, is so incredibly interesting to me from a character standpoint, especially in the way his very dysfunctional love for blackbeard acts as a counter to stede's. I just think it's interesting to see an example where the right hand man has a lot of power over a very vulnerable 'boss'. Here's a hot take on a Right-hand Man: Holmes and Watson! The archetypal Dynamic Duo, yes, but also:  1) Perfect fit for the Brains-and-Brawn category, while Holmes is no slouch and Watson is anything but a dullard, this just serves to up the badassery of both of them, as they exceed each other's high standards that they hold for themselves  2) Obviously deathly loyal on both ends, but also Holmes is basically Watson's employer, given the narrator setting and plot device. It's quite possible, in fact, that the tales of Holmes' exploits might be a steady and reliable source of income for the retired army doctor. So there is also an explicit boss/employee angle.  3) Holmes is shown to rely on Watson time and again, for autopsies, or marksmanship, or general application of violence, or, you know, mending wounds, which is quite essential in the line of work; Whereas Watson is decidedly more self-sufficient, because without Holmes he's just a retired gentleman with an uncannily sharp pen. So, there is emphasis on the boss being more reliant on the right-hand man than the other way round.

One of my favorite instances of Dragons has to be Hunter x Hunter with its Chimera Ant arc, with the three bodyguards of the King. Going to try and avoid spoilers but its interesting as each is a dragon, representing a major physical threat to the protagonists that they have to overcome, and each having an undying loyalty to the king. The interesting part is that each really have a different view on what exactly "undying loyalty to the king" means.   Pitou is loyal to the king as a person, and thus when asked to do something by him for highly personal reasons he does so without hesitations because he recognizes that it is important to the king. In contrast Pouf is loyal to the idea of the king, or rather what he thinks the king should be. And thus goes behind hi back and has plans to remove things he considers getting in the way of what the king should be. And then finally Youpi is loyal to the king because its his job, no more and no less. He doesn't have any great personal connection with the king like Pitou but also isn't obsessed with the king being a certain thing like Pouf, as far as he is concerned he is the king and he is the bodyguard and that's all that matters. This difference in loyalty is best shown when the king asks them all what his name is. Pouf responds he doesn't need a name because he is "The King" and that alone describes what he is. Youpi responds that the question is outside of his abilities to answer. And Pitou responds that the King should choose whatever name he like the most.    In short the three royal guards are a fascinating case of dragons as each have their own definition of loyalty and each show how being loyal to something or someone can take many different forms, and where they go as characters is each informed by there views on loyalty. Pitou learning a certain level of empathy, Pouf going off the deep end, and Youpi gaining a sense of honor.
I feel the trope talks are so in-depth that it could make it a lot easier to come up with a novel idea by testing and twisting these tropes. You take what works and twist in some things that can subvert these expectations to make a more captivating story.

My favorite use of the right hand man trope is actually a pair of them: Zoro and Sanji from One Piece. Zoro is the more normal kind, in the sense that he's the second strongest character, and also the physical support in cases where Luffy needs it. But, Sanji is more of an emotional support (support is often via Sanji's world-class cooking ability) right hand man, though he's no slouch himself when it comes to fighting. They cover each other's weaknesses as well, with Sanji's smart planning balancing out Zoro's moronic moments (see: any time Zoro has to navigate an area on his own) and Zoro's pure strength and insane pain tolerance stepping up when Sanji can't. Add in their hilarious rivalry, and these two are fantastic right hand men.

One heroic bodyguard Trope I really like is the dependent bodyguard. This is usually only seen when the bodyguard is the muscle to a smart guy, but it's interesting to see how some bodyguards grow dependent on having their choices made for them and when they're seperated from their bosses, temperarily or permanently, they have to figure out how to do things by themselves.  Fav example is Akatsuki from Log Horizon.

Owen from Gargoyles is something of a subversion of the standard trope.  He's Xanatos's loyal subordinate and comes about a close to a friend as one can get in such a hierarchy, but he's not particularly badass.  He can fight - he's Xanatos's sparring partner for martial arts practice - but he rarely ever actually uses that ability.  Mostly, he's the extremely reliable and hypercompetent personal secretary, reminding Xanatos of appointments, managing the business side of things while Xanatos is busy going full megalomaniac, etc...but there's an edge to him that you rarely see in these characters.  When he wants to, he presents himself as having absolute authority - in some cases even more so than Xanatos himself.  (Mostly because the latter tends to revel in situations whereas Owen is just....cold, efficient, all business.

An interesting take on this trope occurs in Fire Emblem: Three Houses with two of the house leaders, Dimitri and Edelgard, in which while Dedue serves the classic kind of dynamic to Dimitri after he was saved from a massacre in response to the Tragedy of Duscur, Hubert is more of the clever, scheming behind-the-scenes guy who devotes his loyalty as an advisor to Edelgard. While Edelgard is an intelligent tactician and skilled leader in her own right, she mainly does more of the outwards in the open "big guy" things like leading her troops into the battlefield, whereas Hubert is mainly a chessmaster who does more of the dirty, underhanded and clandestine stuff needed to realize Edelgard's goals. This contrast makes even more sense in gameplay, with Edelgard being an incredibly strong fighter in melee combat, while Hubert is the mage.  As a result, Dimitri and Edelgard contrast each other not only in character, goals, beliefs, and ideals, but also in relation to their right-hand men.

Hellsing's betrayal by Walter against the Hellsing organization is something I find fascinating, and feeds into its larger themes. Indeed, part of why I like to call Hellsing an "Exploitation Opera"; like an Exploitation Film (with its gratuitous violence, sleaziness, and Nazis), but given an operatic tone, scale, and emotional complexity. Characters go on grand, Shakespearean soliloquies about their motivations, meditating on philosophy and their role as heroes or monsters.  When we first meet Walter, he's a loyal bodyguard to Integra, a friend to Alucard, and a grandfatherly mentor to Saras. But as we learn more about him, we learn (and see) he was/is a badass vampire hunter in his own right. That his facade as a genteel English servant belies a capacity for violence, a contempt for his enemies, and even an arrogance.  When his betrayal is revealed, we learn that he, like the Nazi vampires of Millennium, has a desire to either gain another victory in battle or die trying. That he was a cocky, vampire-hunting warrior since his formative youth, and that all these emotional attachments to the protagonists is a NEW thing. That he was planning to betray Hellsing for years, possibly since WWII or sometime after. And while he does, indeed, go through with the plan, it's obvious he didn't expect to feel as bad about betraying his friends. Especially Saras, who was _really_ new to his life, and still thanked him for what he did for her.   While Alucard was ready to give his friend the fight he badly wanted (even if he ultimately had nothing but contempt for traitors), and Integra ordered Walter be killed like any other enemy (even if doing so visibly pained her), Saras still treated him like the mentor he was to her. A response that both took Walter off guard and made him respond with sincerity in kind.  Again, like Millennium, Walter was a relic of an earlier age, a killing machine that desired fighting and victory for its own sake. Beating Alucard was his driving motivation. But so was his desire not to fade away and be forgotten. He would rather become an ignoble villain in this Tragic play - the traitor deserving of a traitor's death - than wither to nothing. And as befits a Tragedy, his attempt fails and his betrayal was for nothing. _He_ ultimately failed to kill Alucard, Millennium "killed" him. Walter gained no satisfaction from it all. And ultimately he died, his only redemption coming from ensuring that no trace of Millennium would go on to trouble the world in the future.

One part of this trope that is VERY IMPORTANT and I think Red overlooked is that it's possible to have multiple Right-Hand Men. Two examples come to mind. Thanos from Marvel, and Sheev Palpatine from Star Wars.  Sheev is much more complicated, so we'll start with Thanos first. Thanos throughout his time in the MCU has had 3-4 Dragons. The first 2 we see are from *The Avengers:* Loki (my beloved) and The Other. In *Guardians of the Galaxy* we get introduced to the radical and zealous Ronan the Accuser, who then promptly offs The Other. Finally we had Ebony Maw, who acts as Thanos' hype-man for the entirety of Infinity War and as his chief scientist for one scene in Endgame. Yes, technically he is Thanos' son, but he is loyal to a fault.  Sheev has Darth Vader of course, but he also has the Sith Inquisition that mysteriously everyone seems to expect. He also has the Empire's own CO Team 6 in the form of the Inferno Squadron.

Ooh, I think I can come up with a reason why the RHM is called the Dragon and it stems back from the example from the Xanatos Gambit page. The villain gets challenged to combat and loses, so they set the dragon on the heroes and before the heroes can finish the villain off, they get eaten by the dragon. With a small bit of retooling at the end, the heroes get defeated or stalled by the Dragon and the villain escapes to survive or wins. The interesting thing with this is the fact that it also can be interpreted as the destructive arm of the main villain, they are sent in for scorched earth tactics and when you want to leave nobody alive or at least strike fear into the hearts of everyone who does manage to escape.

Pearl is not in love with Rose. She has complicated feelings and is obsessed with her. Their relationship with Rose was of Master and "technically-not-a-slave", After a single pale Rose it became clear that the flashback in Rose's Scabbard wasn't of a rejected love confession. I think the "Dragon" trope comes from what I've observed in a lot of classic stories that the Big Villain usually has Someone/something particularly powerful on a leash. In a lot of fantasy stories this typically takes the form of an ACTUAL Dragon. Like the guard to the Bad guy's evil castle, the weapon of mass destruction ready at a snap of their fingers, the powerful ally with shares goals etc.  Take mythical examples like the Dragon Fafnir of Norse legends. A lot of versions frame this Dragon as one of Loki's many devious creations to screw with the other Gods, but most notably in the tale of Sigurd. Where Loki charges Fafnir to stop Sigurd from completing his quest to become the greatest hero in the land and wake Brynhildr from her eternal sleep.
I was rewatching FMA:B recently, and saw the Mustang fights Scar in the rain, and gets saved by Hawkeye. And I‚Äôm kinda convinced that Roy charging in confidently only to have his power fail him wasn‚Äôt part of the plan. Scar is extremely cautious. He‚Äôs not going to let Hawkeye get a clean shot in. But he COULD be lured out of position with the threat and promise of a fight with a very prominent state alchemist. Roy charging him for the attack forces Scar to put all his focus on Mustang, both the stay alive AND because Scar wants to kill state alchemists. So he stops paying attention to Hawkeye for long enough for her to get into a prime position. I think this is intentional, not Roy forgetting his alchemy doesn‚Äôt work in the rain like a dummy. They then play it off because Hawkeye can‚Äôt resist the dig, and Roy still is demoralized by being ineffective in the rain, even if he knew that was going to be the case. Look at when he ducks - how did he know that would work? Because he knew Hawkeye was there.

I think something you missed out on is when the Right-Hand is killed in a dynamic where the person they're protecting actually cares about them. It can shake up a hero's world view and/or have them commit morally wrong actions/become antagonists. And it can create an "oh no" moment when the Big Bad gets off their throne to reveal just how powerful they are, and begin targeting characters specifically whereas before they didn't view the heroes as worth dealing with. Or it turns out that the Right-Hand was the only thing keeping the Big Bad tied to sanity and they throw caution to the wind, not caring for their own safety to achieve their goals or shifting their goals to a "burn the whole world" plan. Shout-out to the villain and right-hand-man in Air Gear, a cool part of an otherwise bizarre manga. Sora and Nike are twin brothers, with Sora being the master planner main villain with surprise power of his own, and Nike being his dragon. It‚Äôs established that Nike is more powerful than Sora, and very intelligent in his own right, but he is subservient to Sora out of both brotherly affection and deeply-ingrained fear toward Sora.  During his final battle, Nike is confronted with the option to either see the light or realize he‚Äôs been wronged by Sora, but rather than go by the tropes and betray Sora in the end, he bends to sunk-cost fallacy and concludes that he‚Äôs done too many villainous things to deserve to go back on it all now, and it‚Äôs this mental break between his drive and doubts that leaves him vulnerable and ultimately taken out of the fight. Sora, meanwhile, is merely disappointed that his pawn has been taken out, erasing any possibility of the trope leading to the boss caring for their right hand.  Also the final battle between Nike and the lancer of the hero group is the peak of that bizarre series, and is way more satisfying than anything the actual main character is undergoing with Sora.

Idea: Classic Evil Emperor Boss hellbent on world domination, Right Hand Man is the second in command and totally believes the propaganda. The Boss sees their RHM as a love interest but can't show it in any obvious ways because of the employment dynamic, so the RHM assumes that they're just being rewarded for their loyalty. Eventually the rebel heroes convince the RHM that world domination does not equal world peace, then the RHM stabs the Boss in the back, the Boss then responds "I still love you" and the RHM puts 2 & 2 together and realizes that their boss was constantly showing them affection, just not in a way that would derail the government. Now the RHM becomes a violent force of nature trying to kill the heroes for tricking him into killing their Boss who actually loved them.

A villain/dragon dynamic I really enjoy is the 'long suffering servant' where the dragon visibly dislikes the villain but puts up with it for personal reasons. It's another way to make a tragic dragon by having them be so clearly better than the villain they serve and suffering for their loyalty.  An example that comes to mind is Wen Xhuliu & Wen Chao in in Mo Dao Zu Shi/The Untamed. Wen Chao is such a piece of garbage that Wen Xhuliu has obvious contempt for and you're waiting for the moment he turns on him, but he never does and ends up dying for his loyalty.
The 1983- 1986 GI Joe series was INSANE with using Right-hand-betrayals as a plot device.  It was a crutch for the entire series basically.  Most of Cobra Commander's plans were absolutely stupid, but those that were actually good and had a chance of working also happened to exclude Destro and/or the Baroness, so they would often find a way to get vital information to the Joes to stop Cobra Commander, and reinforce his reliance on his right hands, and it served as a regular in road for the episode to wrap up in one 22-minute shot and for status quo to be maintained.  Destro is DEFINETELY one of those "The only thing the villain has going for him is me" types.  If he were the one doing the planning, the series might have ended in the first 5-part miniseries, with Destro winning LOL

Spoilers for anime watchers of Kaguya-sama: love is war         The series Love is war provides a more interesting take of the betrayal of right hand-man with Ai hayasaka and Kaguya Shinomiya. Essentially Ai hayasaka was planted by Kaguya‚Äôs eldest big brother to watch and report to him  whatever she does, but as time passed  she developed a sister-like relationship with kaguya and gained tremendous guilt in continuing her secret so much that she quit her job as Kaguya‚Äôs personal maid. But When the third big brother captured Hayasaka and revealed her betrayal to Kaguya, in order to have an alliance with Kaguya to remove the eldest, instead of doing what she thought to do by her family and never forgive traitors, Kaguya breaks the family norms and asks Hasaka to help her learn to forgive her
Only semi-relevant to the video and not sure if anyone else is like this, but when I see villains have good guy traits (overcoming legit struggles, caring about the people around them), then my gut reaction is to consider them more threatening as a villain. Good guy traits like overcoming adversity and the power of friendship hit my brain as "traits of those who win", so when they clash with the actual good guys, I'm usually much more invested.  Like when I see Kira from jojo have his own encounters and winning after being put in dire situations, I take him more seriously in the final fight because he's been in struggles like it before and won. In contrast, Dio is above it all and acts like he's the perfect and untouchable being, so when his pride is damaged or he is bested even a little bit then the rest of the fight feels more like a decline to the inevitable defeat.  Also when characters like the Phantom Troupe from Hunter x Hunter or the 7 bandits from Inuyasha give a shit about each other, then when one is killed by the good guys there is this burst of motivation that make them more intense and seem more threatening for their respective fights.
You know what could be interesting? If instead of the Dragon having a redemption arc, their boss joins the good guys first and leaves them behind, turning the Dragon into the new serious threat now that they're personally motivated by feelings of betrayal and no longer just a second in command. Also a fun way to implement this sub-trope is if the former Dragon showing up to terrorize the heroes causes them to realize that supporting character with a dark, mysterious past used to be one of the bad guys

I think the term Dragon to speak of a vilain's right-hand man comes from the movie "The Patriot" with Mel Gibson, though I could be wrong. In the movie, which takes place during the American war of independance, main character Benjamin Martin leads a small militia unit to fight against the forces of British general Cornwalls whose been task with supressing the rebellion. However, Martin's rivallry and conflict isn't with the general but with one of his high ranking officers, Colonel Tavington who leads the dragoons regiment. Now, dragoon is a baby dragon but it is also a type of light infantry that fought with single shot pistols and one-handed sabers during the 17th and 18th centuries. Thhey're sort of an evolution of the lancers after things like kngihts and spear have become obselite. I unfortunately can't find any source to confirm or debuke this theory though

Interestingly, "The Boys" turns many of these tropes on their ear, in a spectacular fashion.
Dr Aphra fits this trope pretty nicely in the 2015 Darth Vader comic. She's a space archeologist, a sort of Indiana Jones type if he had a very broken moral compass. Vader is kind of on the outs with the Emperor for that whole: The Death Star exploded and you are the only one alive I can blame; thing. Aphra provides Vader with tech support, robotic minions and other connections in the underworld. She also bounces of Vader very well, since he is quite taciturn and stoic while she talks to much and snarks. Dr Aphra starts out as a bit of fangirl for Vader and seeks some sort of validation from him, a purpose to her very shitty life, while also being terrified that he'll kill her if she stops being useful.   MAJOR SPOILERS from here:   This ends quite badly as after Aphra screws up a job, she's certain Vader is going to kill her (which he probably is). So she decides her best course of action is to BACKSTAB VADER, by telling on him to the Emperor. This is the first shown case of what I like to call Backstabirites, a condition that makes you backstab everyone. The dragon is probably called that on tv tropes because they fill the role of the dragon controlled by the evil wizard and such in archetypical fantasy stories.
A personal favourite variation of the bodyguard betrayal is the Bodyguard betraying their Bosses ideal or goal with the intent of still saving their boss.  For example selling out their Boss's supper risky hidden scheme to make it crash and burn prematurely and to allow both the Bodyguard and Boss to walk away cleanly, in theory at least.  The space between personal loyalty and loyalty to a cause is a gold mine for  potential character drama and development.

All this talk about "right hand [men]" has overlap with the advisor and king trope, the "power behind the throne," if you will.  This is a mutable inversion of the trope.  The advisor knows the leader on the throne is very valuable, as the advisor has neither a legitimate claim, nor the willingness to impractically place a target on their back.  The king is used as a mouthpiece to perform the advisor's plans.  If the advisor is  cartoonishly evil and ambitious, he'll scheme and act to usurp the king. Heroic advisors would be performing similar schemes, though the plot (and therefore context) drives all schemes an advisor would ever make.

One of my favorite Tragic Dragon to Villain relationships...Arthur Morgan to Dutch Van Der Linde. Looking at it from the perspective that these are the bad guys (because they are) Arthur looks to Dutch like a father figure (flawed as he is) while Dutch only calls Arthur his son but his actions treat Arthur like a typical attack dog. It's a obvious from an outside point of view but it's one of those relationships where they do care about each other in a way that adds that juicy depth to their relationship.
Some classic mentor/teacher and student relationships also use this trope as well because usually the mentor/teacher despite being smarter and wiser than the student needs them because they are usually too old to fight or crippled with some sort illness or injury.   The mentor/teacher fits with the smart and usually weaker boss character who leads the right hand man or the student in this case in their journey while setting them on the right path and the student fits with the right hand man trope of being their protector and trying their best to make sure they get out their journey alive, Sometimes they might even develop a different arc apart from the ones in this video where the mentor has come to realize that their student might even be their successor when they are gone and probably develop a parent-child relationship with them.   Also, When you talked about how the relationships between villains and their right hand men would go. I was instantly reminded of Netflix‚Äôs Castlevania with the relationship between Dracula and Varney.   Where it completely flips our impression and expectation of them right on its head when we thought Dracula is gonna be this big and scary final boss type villain that the heroes have to defeat in order to end the series because he is the one who started all of this mess   Until we find out that he becomes a pretty tragic and sympathetic character who abandons his evil goals and plans before the series is near its end.   When we got to meet Varney for the first time, Most of us thought he was going to be a starscream-like incompetent and arrogant right hand man waiting to run away from the heroes once they start to close in on him and have Dracula beat the ever loving tar out of him whenever he sees him failing.   And then we got to see the twist where it is revealed that he is not only the true main antagonist and villain of the series but he is the most powerful character in the series who is powerful enough to overpower and outrank Dracula himself.   The reason why Varney helped Dracula in the first place is to reap the rewards of his personal war and to become even more powerful.   This is literally the opposite of the trope where the main villains sees their right hand man as nothing but a valuable tool at their disposal ready to get rid off once they have no use for them.   Varney who is the right hand man in this situation sees Dracula who is the seemingly main antagonist and villain in the exact same way despite our first impressions of them.   Varney sees Dracula who is the most feared character in the series as nothing but a pushover and his ticket to reaching his own goals despite their appearances.

This prompts me to pick apart the Fellowship of the Ring in terms of Right-Hand Man and Five-Man Band.  Sam, of course, is Frodo's Right-Hand Hobbit, since he's committed to keeping Frodo safe, though in so far as he has an "official" function, it's more as valet.  The whole Fellowship might be regarded as an extended Five (Plus)-Man Band:  If Frodo's the "leader" (i.e. central character, though he doesn't conventionally lead), then Sam is the "lancer" (of a very non-competitive type), Gandalf is the "smart guy," Aragorn, Boromir, and Gimli are "big guys," and Legolas, Merry, and Pippin are "chicks" (mainly emotional barometers, of wildly different levels of bad-assery).  If Gandalf is the "leader" (more the way the situation is regarded in-story), then Aragorn is the "lancer," Boromir, Legolas, and Gimli are "big guys," with the "smart guy" function subsumed by Gandalf himself, and the hobbits are a chorus of "chicks." 6:00 or so: They're called the 'dragon' because it was once a common fantasy trope for the evil wizard character to have a literal dragon that enforced their will. The basic idea is that the dragon has immense power to the degree they could perhaps take out their boss but the boss is what's directing that immense power. It fits even when the dragon is their own character so the term stuck.

I gotta give a shout out to how one of my favorite instances of Villain - dragon was written.  Zabuza and Haku from Naruto.  A major theme running through that storyline was "A ninja SHOULD bury their feelings and only focus on their mission." as well as posing the question, "Is it possible for any ninja to actually live up to that idea?"   In the final showdown, our loyal dragon, Haku puts aside everything to die for the sake of his master Zabuza's mission.  At first, our heartless villain acts like it's no major loss.  Haku was just a lackey.  Nothing more, and if he had to die for the mission, so be it. Ninja's arn't meant to care or get emotional. There's stuff to be done. But as he's pushed harder and harder, clearly about to loose everything, Zabuza slowly breaks down.  Not because it's the end of the line for him. It's because he can't stop thinking about Haku.  He never realized until it was too late just how much he cared about his right hand man and all of his regrets even in the face of death involve how much he wished Haku could have had better.

Definitely influenced by recency bias, but What We Do in the Shadows (the TV show) works as a great example of this played for laughs, almost to the degree of parody. Guillermo, a villainous right-hand man carrying out or setting up a lot of consistent casual murder for the benefit of the rest of the cast, is essentially the protagonist of the series. His status as a familiar is his defining characteristic to every other character and how they interact with him, and they manage to use most if not every listed relationship twist when it comes to his master, Nandor. At the very least each option (betrayal, abandonment, a romantic relationship) is considered specifically from Guillermo's perspective. The dark comedic genre is a good way to have it all. 1:00 - I've observed this many stories. Even in series where it isn't readily apparent from the start, later stories will reveal more things that make the protagonist further stand out and apart from everyone else.   But, in some cases, it can feel like the creator is trying to emphasize the "main character-ness" of the protagonist over and over. E.g. in a world of magic users where the protagonist is already established as a rare talent at the start,  something is further revealed later in the series (bloodline, prophecy, unique powers, cosmically significant times of birth, etc.) to further make them stand out from anyone they shared their starting specialness with; to emphasize why the plot revolves around the main character.  It arguably makes the scenarios where such things don't matter as much, where the social bonds and ensemble cast have more weight to the progression of the story, stand out.

What I find most interesting about this trope is that the right-hand man's character is typically dependent on the boss's character to give it meaning, so while the boss can continue to remain relevant to the plot on their own, the right-hand man can't, not without intrinsically tying their relationship to the boss in some manner, with the exception being the betrayer who takes on the role of main villan after back-stabbing their boss.

I suspect the reason the evil "lancer" is called the "dragon" is because the "lancer" typically fights the "dragon". And one way to fight a dragon (or any large creature in a fantasy setting) is to charge it down with a lance.

Yeah, we all know the anti-memes about how he's the single most powerful character in the Fellowship, but I think his primary defining trait is his loyalty to Frodo. It isn't really established why he's loyal, he just is.   And that loyalty plays a very interesting role in the story, as Sam really was a major contributor to the destruction of the Ring, having rescued Frodo from Cirith Ungol and being the main voice of reason to help shake Frodo out of his increasingly eroding, Ring-obsessed mental state.

My favorite version of this trope is when it is mixed with the reformed villain trope.
Two of my favourite examples of this trope are from RWBY and Miraculous Ladybug. In RWBY you have Roman Torchwick and Neo, a pair of criminals who serve the bad guys of the first three seasons. Roman is the boss while Neo is the Right Hand Man, and they have a very fun dynamic of teasing each other like close friends would. There‚Äôs no romance involved, it‚Äôs just two close friends committing crime, until Roman is killed at the end of the third season and Neo ends up going on a vengeance quest to avenge his death. In Miraculous, you have Gabriel and Natalie. Natalie works for Gabriel, who‚Äôs secretly series big bad Hawkmoth/ShadowMoth, which makes it a professional relationship. She serves him because he pays her, that‚Äôs it. However, she begins to fall in love with him, caring about him so deeply that she even tries using a broken Miraculous to save him from being defeated by the good guys, which gets dangerous when she continues to use it. On Gabriel‚Äôs side, while she is just his assistant and partner in crime, he never takes her for granted and always shows that he both cares about her and desires her advice.
I think my favorite use of this trope is when the boss is temporarily taken out of action and the right hand man now has to do both jobs. this can lead them to believe they should just take over, or make them realize they don''t like the boss' goals, or even realize they don't have the skill set to be totally in charge and reinforce their loyalty
Another villainous variation (though not always) is when the Left Hand Man is keeping the villain in check.  Maybe they were their morale compass. Or they were the litteral key, while the villain had immense evil sealed inside them.  ANd now, they're gone and the previously inept villain is going to destroy reality as we know it. Good Job. Or our heroes knows this before hand/learned it just before the final battle, and have to find another way to deal with the Big Bad. Maybe defeating it first. Maybe  try to talk them out of whatever evil they were planning. Or just bonk them with a heavy object while we deal with the big bad, though that's less interesting.

There's also an interesting sub variant of the Dragon Ascendant trope in which the main villain goes through a reception arc and abandons their evil scheme(s), only to be backstabbed and replaced by their Dragon, who it turns out was way more committed to the whole evil thing. This has the benefit of making the final villain someone that the hero(es) already have a history with, but risks disappointing the audience if they were hoping for a showdown with the original villain. It's also a pretty handy trope in games, as it can allow the player to successfully diplomance the main villain into submission without sacrificing the exciting boss fight.

Mo Dao Zu Shi has the right-hand man dynamic in several areas of the story, the most notable is Wen Chow and Wen Zhuliu. Now unlike many dynamics presented, Wen Chow is the second son of a powerful lord. He may be the boss when he is on his own, but he is also pretty ordinary when compared to a lot of the main characters of the story and very much the low man on the power dynamics when it comes to his own family. However his dad is the big bad, at least at the start of the story, and he has no qualms about using the threat of his daddy's power to lord over other people, Wen Zhuliu is his bodyguard and loyal to the clan to a fault. Now Wen Zhuliu has a unique and terrifying power that makes him a badass in contrast with Wen Chow's own lack of said. What is really interesting about them is that no matter how messed up Wen Chow gets in his actions, Wen Zhuliu doesn't betray him, even when he *knows* what is happening is messed up and wrong. Even if he personally sees the wrong his loyalty to the clan, to Wen Chow's father, won't allow him to let his charge come to harm if he can prevent it.  When confronted by another character about protecting Chow, Zhuliu replies that he must repay his gratitude to Chow's father. In response, Wei Wuxian demands to know why other people had to suffer in order for him to pay for his personal debts. The dynamic is fascinating and I highly recommend it.

The Right Hand Man trope makes me think of Eve from No Straight Roads. I feel it's a worthy blueprint for Right Hand Men/Women worth discussing.  Before she met Tatiana, her boss, she was a girl who clearly had... _issues._ She was desperate to find people who saw the world the way that she did. After her boyfriend left her when she illuminated his hair with bright lights (e.g. setting his hair on fire), she became afraid that there was no one that could. She began working to spread her own popularity as an artist. Tatiana took notice, and welcomed her into the No Straight Roads company.  Eve and Tatiana demonstrate a 3d relationship throughout their appearances. According to Eve herself, Tatiana accepted her for who she was. But it's not a controlling relationship- she doesn't feel too uncomfortable to question Tatiana's orders when they hit personally. She even felt comfortable enough to bring her TV over to Tatiana's place to watch the news and understandably was miffed when her boss punches it after seeing the protagonists' success.  It's a really weird example of a Right Hand Man. But what I think should be taken away from it is that there's a lot to be gained from the Right Hand Man or Woman being their own character with their own personality, wants, and history, especially if those contextualize why they have the relationship that they have with their boss.

I think one of the more unique examples of the heroic "right hand man" trope is from "The Hit-mans Bodyguard." Ryan Reynolds character Michael Brice is tasked with protecting his sworn enemy, the hit man Darius Kincaid. Played by Samuel L. Jackson.  Bryce has to protect Darius, because The Government needs him and have made a deal. Meanwhile, Darius has to keep Bryce alive for his own safety and benefit.  Which is fun because it puts the heroes in a sort of antagonist-like relationship. Because, for all Intent and purposes they would gladly fight each other in any other circumstance. But right now they can't so they show their hatred for each other as insults and belittling each other.  Its essentially Heroic or hero adjacent characters being put in a typically villains only situation.

5:43 Just a thought, but maybe it's called "The Dragon" as a reference to the disney-style fairy tales, the ones where you have some evil magic user that imprisons the princess that's then guarded by a dragon. Sleeping Beauty is the first one that comes to mind as an actual example, Shrek is another one, but the trope of "imprisoned princess guarded by a dragon" is common enough in western storytelling (which is probably why Shrek used it in the first place)

I'm very partial to the Right-Hand Man trope, but this trope has one major series that uses it damn well while also having a lot of variety in it's approach. Let's talk Fire Emblem.  I can only talk about Awakening, Fates and Three Houses, but in all three games every member of royalty has 1 to 2 retainers, with both acting as a right-hand man. Awakening has your character become one of the two faithful retainers to a prince of a local kingdom, and you essentially act as the tactician to his badass (despite the fact that Robin is a comically powerful unit) and help him and the kingdom. This is despite the disapproval of the second retainer, Fredrick, because of his natural job in trying to keep the prince safe.  Fire Emblem Fates is kind of an "Oops, All Retainers" story, because each of the 9 main lords in the game have 2 retainers. Each of the retainers are very distinct though, but it also showcases how valued the royalty is to both nations, while also giving the excuse to add a lot more distinct personalities to the group. Honestly, this wasn't my favourite approach, but that's mostly thanks to the poor writing and translation between the versions. Oh well, this still gave us Niles and Odin together.  Three Houses is probably the most interesting because of who specifically doesn't have a retainer. Of the four main lords (being Edelgard who has a Magic retainer in Hubert, Dimitri who has two retainers in Dedue and Gilbert being bodyguard varients, and Rhea who has Seteth as a tactician retainer) Claude is the only one who doesn't technically have a defined one, because he's the only one of the four main lords that doesn't need either a retainer nor the protagonist in his respective route. Dimitri and Edelgard have enough emotional baggage to blatently require the protagonist, and Rhea desires the lead to walk with her. Claude however is set in his goals regardless of the route, but that's because he both has the brains to make plans and brawn to carry it out. He's the only Lord of the four that actually Xanatos' his way through the game. Neat.
one of my favorite scenes for a villain right hand man, was Dr. Eggman and his right hand man in sonic 2, where when eggman got his 3rd act power up, and starts to build the Death Egg, his right hand man, comes out yells "doctor, take me with you" and just picks him up to take him along. which i think is more fun than the whole "i don't need you anymore whahaha"
"The Dragon" as trope name originates in Joseph Campbell's "The Hero Of A Thousand Faces", which compares various fantasy heroes. In folklore, the hero will often fight an *actual* dragon before confronting the intelligent but physically weaker Big Bad. Hence, dragons (or stand-ins for them) are often portrayed as a Big Bad's second-in-command.

On the topic of the "Dragon" name being weird, it's just a classic case of definition drift. TVtropes originally had the term mean a major physical threat the protags would have to go through to reach the (often less mighty in terms of raw power) big bad. So it was called that because it would and could often be just some kind of giant monster (not always, though, see Vader for instance). But then, with time and people trying to shoehorn their favorite shows into every page, the definition went from that to a less specific "the second to last boss" to "the big bad's second-in-command". So yeah, like in most languages the name makes no sense because the sense went away over time.

Berserk has one of my favorite right hand man relationships between Griffith and Guts.  Griffith is the leader of a mercenary band that climbs its way to full official army status, and Guts is the protaganist and one of the Captains in that band.  Griffith is repeatedly shown to be ruthless and willing to sacrifice anyone except for Guts, who he risks everything to save over and over.  But Guts wants to be by Griffith's side as a friend, not an underling, so leaves the army.  Griffith sees this as betrayal and abandonment from the one person he ever saw as a friend instead of a tool, setting off his slide from questionably ethical hero to full blown villain, which in time turns Guts against him.  Their dynamic shifts and changes, and all the changes are deeply character driven.

Love this trope? Read One Piece. Zoro (his pirate captain Luffy's right-hand man),  and a fascinating relationship with his captain. Addresses every important question you could ever have and weaves it into the overall short-term *and* long-term  narrative seamlessly. It's very compelling. Zoro has it in his nature to be the boss, and dedicates all his energies into his own goal of becomes the greatest swordsman in the world. He makes a crazy amount of sacrifices to that end... and that's what makes it so compelling when he gradually grows to value his captain's dream even above his own. Luffy's goal, if you don't know, is to become "King of the Pirates." "Pirates" being people who chose to live independent of the totalitarian government of the setting. So, being King of the Independents is a seemingly ludicrous goal. Larger than life, at the very least. That's just one excellent character's arc, too. Read One Piece. It's a long commitment, but 100% worth your time.

Potential Trope Talk: The Last of their Kind. So many ways to approach this topic. From the noble hero that represents ideals long gone and a morality that is implied to be dying in the modern era, to the evil of the past coming back to an unprepared era, to even honest conversations about the evils of the past decimating entire civilizations and the preservation of culture such as protecting isolated people like the North Sentinelese.
